<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_26117706-f4a4-4bb3-9300-e2e55cb0ba23'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

        if (this.up('#box')) {

  <span id="freeText6575921122967684970" style="display:none">Anne's children are almost grown up, except for pretty, high-spirited Rilla. No one can resist her bright hazel eyes and dazzling smile. Rilla, almost fifteen, can't think any further ahead than going to her very first dance at the Four Winds lighthouse and getting her first kiss from handsome Kenneth Ford. But undreamed-of challenges await the irrepressible Rilla when the world of Ingleside is endangered by a far-off war. Her brothers go off to fight, and Rilla brings home an orphaned newborn in a soup tureen. She is swept into a drama that tests her courage and changes her forever.</span>

<a href="/questions/1029092-is-this-book-going-to-make-me-cry-the">Is this book going to make me cry?

The other Anne of Green Gables Chronicles are my favorite books in the world, and I&#39;ve been studiously avoiding this one for years. But I think the time has at last come.

<div id="review_1401301" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1401301" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/94075-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1376137786p2/94075.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1401301?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/94075-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1401301" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6094033931681191048">This is the final book of the Anne series, and deals with the lives of her children. While it's wonderful to see characters evolve into adulthood and have a next generation come to life, the real strength of this book is it's window into the homeland society of rural Maritime Canada during the first World War. It's one of few documentations (even if the characters are fictional) of what women were doing at home during the war, and this setting breathes new life into the end of the series. The se</span>
  <span id="freeText6094033931681191048" style="display:none">This is the final book of the Anne series, and deals with the lives of her children. While it's wonderful to see characters evolve into adulthood and have a next generation come to life, the real strength of this book is it's window into the homeland society of rural Maritime Canada during the first World War. It's one of few documentations (even if the characters are fictional) of what women were doing at home during the war, and this setting breathes new life into the end of the series. The setting also allows Montgomery to challenge readers with darker elements that are often present but less overt in other books. Rilla, Anne's youngest daughter, is a wonderful, spirited character and one of my favorites. <br /><br />In general, Montgomery's work is constantly under-estimated, and the way the books are marketed doesn't help (the flowery script, the swoony illustrations). There are many layers at work in her stories, and some pioneering feminist concepts tucked in between the deep appreciation of nature, the commentary on the stuffy contemporary society of her day, and the delightful, well-drawn characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6094033931681191048" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lindsay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lindsay" href="/user/show/2701485-lindsay">Lindsay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149570477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13041122859499750995">This is the book that finally severed my relationship with Kevin Sullivan. When <i>Anne of Green Gables: The Continuing Story</i> premiered in the early 2000s, I was shocked to discover that Anne and Gilbert were suddenly time warped to WWI. <br/><br/>That's wrong. On so many levels.<br/><br/>WWI wasn't Anne's war, it was Rilla's, and Ken's, and Walter's, and that damn dog who turns me into a gushy mess every freaking time I read the last chapter. Rilla -- the youngest child of Anne and Gilbert -- is fifteen (see what I m</span>
  <span id="freeText13041122859499750995" style="display:none">This is the book that finally severed my relationship with Kevin Sullivan. When <i>Anne of Green Gables: The Continuing Story</i> premiered in the early 2000s, I was shocked to discover that Anne and Gilbert were suddenly time warped to WWI. <br /><br />That's wrong. On so many levels.<br /><br />WWI wasn't Anne's war, it was Rilla's, and Ken's, and Walter's, and that damn dog who turns me into a gushy mess every freaking time I read the last chapter. Rilla -- the youngest child of Anne and Gilbert -- is fifteen (see what I mean about the time warp??) and desperately in love with the boy next door. When the world around her crashes and she's forced to grow up quickly, her journey is just as enjoyable as her mother's was in the first three books. The difference is, this is a mature kind of love. Though I devoured the entire series as a seven year old, <i> Rilla of Ingleside </i> is the one that I've worn through with repeated readings as an adult. Simply put, it's perfection.<br /><br />The fact that Kevin Sullivan clearly ignored the fact that this book was written at all is frustrating and, to a reader who considers this book one of the reasons she became a romance writer, downright cruel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13041122859499750995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jess the Shelf-Declared Bibliophile" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2744708-jess-the-shelf-declared-bibliophile"><img alt="Jess the Shelf-Declared Bibliophile" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606058182p2/2744708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1404092164?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess the Shelf-Declared Bibliophile" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess the Shelf-Declared Bibliophile" href="/user/show/2744708-jess-the-shelf-declared-bibliophile">Jess the Shelf-Declared Bibliophile</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1404092164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10837559288358873337">I will confess, this one took me a little while to get into, likely because the last book was so enjoyable reading about the exhorts of the older children. Rilla wasn't especially close to my heart when starting this book. I'm happy to report that this book soared above all expectations once I pushed through a little bit in the beginning, and it became one of my favorites of the series. The sadness, fear and dread of World War 1 in the background against the day to day life in Prince Edward Isla</span>
  <span id="freeText10837559288358873337" style="display:none">I will confess, this one took me a little while to get into, likely because the last book was so enjoyable reading about the exhorts of the older children. Rilla wasn't especially close to my heart when starting this book. I'm happy to report that this book soared above all expectations once I pushed through a little bit in the beginning, and it became one of my favorites of the series. The sadness, fear and dread of World War 1 in the background against the day to day life in Prince Edward Island was of a much more somber note than the other books, but it was worth it. I don't remember crying while reading any of the other books of the series, but I cried so hard in this one I could hardly see the words. My favorite part of the book, however, has to go to Dog Monday. <br /><br />Anyone who has ever been a fan of Anne of Green Gables, the first book OR the BBC series, I would strongly recommend you read the series at some point! You won't regret it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10837559288358873337" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

This story has a more serious tone than the other books but it is a fitting conclusion to a wonderful story.</span>

<div id="review_2270990492" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2270990492" />
    <a title="E.F.B." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39244832-e-f-b"><img alt="E.F.B." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424876183p2/39244832.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2270990492?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="E.F.B." class="user" itemprop="url" name="E.F.B." href="/user/show/39244832-e-f-b">E.F.B.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Lovers of WWI fiction</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=books-i-ll-read-again">books-i-ll-read-again</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=made-me-cry-in-a-good-way">made-me-cry-in-a-good-way</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=made-me-smile">made-me-smile</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2270990492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6100468806732196616">I started reading the Anne of Green Gables series for the first time in late 2016. Slowly, but surely, I made my way through. I liked some of the books and loved others, but none of them ever quite match the first book, which remained my favorite of them all. Then, along came Rilla. I knew from certain Goodreads friends that it was different from the rest of the books because it's focus is on Anne's daughter rather than Anne herself. What I didn't realize was how very emotional it would be (in a</span>
  <span id="freeText6100468806732196616" style="display:none">I started reading the Anne of Green Gables series for the first time in late 2016. Slowly, but surely, I made my way through. I liked some of the books and loved others, but none of them ever quite match the first book, which remained my favorite of them all. Then, along came Rilla. I knew from certain Goodreads friends that it was different from the rest of the books because it's focus is on Anne's daughter rather than Anne herself. What I didn't realize was how very emotional it would be (in a good way, actually), because, as the book begins, they are approaching the beginning of WWI. The Great War. In all the previous Anne books, I discovered that there was literally ALWAYS one thing in every book sad enough to make me cry, and I had to learn to brace for it. List of some spoilery sad things, just because: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Matthew dying, Anne sitting at Ruby Gillis' bedside and talking about heaven with her as she was dying of consumption, that poor little boy of whom Anne and her friend took a picture and he later died (his dying words to his father were what got me. Wah!), Anne's first baby dying, Captain Jim dying, etc, etc.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />But this was not the case with Rilla. Oh, no. With Rilla, I WAS CRYING THROUGH THE WHOLE THING. I'm not joking, while there were some lighter-hearted chapters thrown in here and there, in general I could barely get through a single chapter without tearing up at least a little, just from the knowledge of how horrible that time in history was, not to mention seeing what it was like for the characters to live during that time (which Iâ€™m sure was extremely realistic given that L. M. Montgomery lived through that time herself), having loved ones going and fighting, not knowing what would happen to them, much less what would happen to the world by the end of the conflict, and being afraid with every phone call that you would be told your loved one wasnâ€™t coming back. <br /><br />This is actually why it took me a full month just to read this book. My main daily reading time is right before bed, but fear of death, actual death, and world war, do not good bedtime reading make, no matter how redemptively the author handles it. It was making me so emotional, I was having trouble sleeping afterwards, and so had to relegate it to daytime reading only. Unfortunately, I don't often get long periods of daytime reading, thus the slow progress. I just wanted to clarify that so no future readers of this review who didnâ€™t see my updates while I read think I took so long because I wasnâ€™t enjoying it, or that it was a difficult read, because I very much did enjoy it, and it was no more difficult to read than the rest of the Anne books. In fact, Iâ€™d say it had a lot fewer superfluous scenes in it than some of the other Anne books did, and I donâ€™t remember skimming at all. Had I been on my normal reading schedule, I probably would have had it finished in under two weeks. <br /><br />That said, Iâ€™ll get on to the things I liked and disliked. <br /><br /><b>Dislikes:</b> â€¦Not much, and the things I did dislike were more personal nit-picks than anything that took away from my reading experience, but I will mention them for the sake of balance. There was one girl Rilla knew who was a major jerk. The type that, if youâ€™re friends with her, everythingâ€™s fine and dandy, but get on her bad side and sheâ€™ll treat you badly and try to get everyone else to turn against you, too. Thankfully, she didnâ€™t feature much, but I was extremely annoyed when she <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ told Rilla, right before the concert, that Walter had enlisted, and also tried to make it seem like he had specifically confided in her instead of Rilla. She did this specifically to upset Rilla so she wouldnâ€™t do well in the concert,  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and I just thought that was the meanest thing. :(<br /><br />The only other things I can think of is that I did sometimes miss getting Anneâ€™s perspective on things. We got glimpses of it here and there, but didnâ€™t spend near as much time in her point of view as we did in other books. Speaking of missing things, I have felt at times that Montgomery had Anne and Gilbert have a few more kids than she, the author, maybe should have. On one hand, she did manage to have the all the children, and many other characters represented at least a little throughout the course of the book. On the other hand, half the Blythe kids got WAY more characterization than the others. Nan and Di especially seemed to just be set dressing in this one. They were off at school most of the time, and even when they were home, they didnâ€™t do much. Shirley got a bit more to do in this book than the last two (I honestly forgot he existed at times, in the last two books), but I never developed any particular attachment to him because all I knew about him was that Susan felt like a mother to him because sheâ€™d had to do so much for him when he was first born because Anne got so sick. Then, the only thing I learned about him in this book was that he felt duty-bound to join the war and fly air planes. Thatâ€™sâ€¦kinda it. Had he died I would have been slightly sad for his familyâ€™s sake, but I canâ€™t say I would have shed tears. Sorry, Shirley. :/<br /><br /><b>Likes:</b> Pretty much everything else! While I did miss Anneâ€™s perspective, this is a childrenâ€™s series, and I understand why Montgomery wanted to focus on the kids again. In this case, Rilla was the baby of the family and had the most growing to do, so I felt that she was a great narrator to choose. She was so frivolous and silly at the beginning of the book and it was wonderful to see her mature and grow so much, but also never become embittered. She was stronger than even she thought she was and rose to the challenges of life again and again. <br /><br />Rilla and Ken. Need I say more? So sweet, so innocent, and it was so cute how Rillaâ€™s lisp would come back around Ken, which just made them more adorable, especially in that very last scene. &lt;3 <br /><br />Susan. Susan was such a â€œbrickâ€ as they called her in the book, and thatâ€™s a good thing! So strong through all of it, running up the flag after every victory, yelling at the Kaiser as if he could actually hear her. I think the family was right when they thought, if the Germans made it to Canada, Susan would face them all down and win. XD Such a wonderful lady who helped keep their spirits lifted through it all. <br /><br />The family dynamics. Iâ€™ve always loved Anne and Gilbertâ€™s family, even if all the kids werenâ€™t characterized equally. Anne and Gilbert still love each other and seem to have a stable marriage in their 50â€™s, and they and the kids all love and support each other. Anne and Gilbert never stopped their children from doing the things they felt they needed to do, namely going to fight in the war and helping with the war effort, even when it meant a personal sacrifice for them. I also appreciated how Gilbert handled Rilla bringing her war baby home. He didnâ€™t want the baby to just get shoved off and Anne and Susan, so he challenged Rilla to either take care of it herself or agree to send it to the asylum, all the while knowing that the baby would stay there at Ingleside no matter what, but also knowing that Rilla needed this opportunity to take responsibility and grow from it. <br /><br />Then there was Rilla and Walterâ€™s relationship, which was so wonderful. I love seeing close sibling relationships in books, especially brother/sister, and Rillaâ€™s love for, and confidence in Walter, and the way they confided in each other was so sweet. And then the letter Walter wrote to Rilla beforeâ€¦beforeâ€¦well, you know before what, if youâ€™ve read the book, and I wonâ€™t spoil you if you havenâ€™t yet. But, the fact that Walter knew what was coming and that he took the time to send Rilla his love and encourage her to keep fighting no matter what. *eyes watering* Oh, Walterâ€¦<br /><br />Dog Monday. WAH! So many tears caused by him. So many poignant moments made even more poignant because of his loyalty in waiting at the train station, and especially how he knew when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Walter was gone, and howled all night, mourning him before the family ever got the news.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> *cries just thinking about it* Not going to get over that any time soon. And then the beauty when he was reunited with his best friend again andâ€¦andâ€¦ *more tears*<br /><br />The analogy of the Pied Piperâ€™s song being the irresistible call to war. Oh boy, did Montgomery ever use that one masterfully. The use of that as foreshadowing in the previous book just made it hit home that much harder in this book, and it was so painful, and yet so beautiful and perfect. I already had great respect for those who fight for good in the armed forces around the world, and this book only served to deepen that respect. To quote the book, â€œLet us drink to the silent armyâ€”to the boys [and these days, the women, too] who followed when the Piper summoned. â€˜For our tomorrow they gave their todayâ€™â€”theirs is the victory!â€<br /><br />The faith element, again, was present and well used. Even though they at times had doubts, the family kept their faith in God through the whole war, knowing that good would overcome evil, even when it didnâ€™t happen as quickly as they hoped, and Montgomery used this very well to show where the hand of God was at work during the war. To quote Mr. Meredith, I truly do believe there were times when God said to the enemy, â€œThis far. No farther.â€ And I believe He continues to do that in many conflicts, both global and personal. <br /><br />Gertrude Oliverâ€™s vivid prediction dreams, while not an element I expected Montgomery to use, especially as even the Blythe family didnâ€™t believe in them at first, provided further spiritual depth and poignancy to certain happenings. The way she dreamed before the war started that there was a tide of blood coming that could not be held back. The time when it was feared that that vital city in France would fall, but she dreamed that even in the midsts of the raging storm, there was the French soldier standing strong against the tide and saying, â€œThey will not pass.â€ And finally, when she dreamed the tide was receding, and the sun would shine again. All so beautiful, so poignant, and all provided some of the most vivid mental images for me while reading the book. <br /><br />This depth of emotion is the biggest reason why Rilla of Ingleside is now tied with Anne of Green Gables as my favorite of the series. I canâ€™t claim itâ€™s overtaken Anne simply from the standpoint that they are such different books, set in such different times in world history, and I truly love them both, just for totally different reasons: I love Anne for its hope, innocence, and determination to find beauty even in the midst of tough circumstances, and I love Rilla for its depth, faith, and a different kind of hope: One that has been through the fire and come out the other side, not untouched, but still strengthened because of what itâ€™s been through. Those are values that mean something to me personally, and for that reason, I am glad to have finally read these books, and will cherish them always.<br /><br /><br /><br /><b>Content advisory for those who want to know: </b><br /><br />Due to the heavy themes of world war and personal loss in this book, I would recommend it for readers 12 and up unless, perhaps, a younger reader is particularly mature and not easily upset by such things. <br /><br /><b>Violence:</b> The horrors of war are a big part of this story, but are never described graphically. We only read brief mentions of injury, such as someone was shot in the leg, someone was knocked out by an exploding shell, someone was killed instantly by a bullet, etc. We also read brief mention of how innocent women and children were starving and/or were killed in the war, and how prisoners of war were treated badly, but again, the treatment is not described. <br /><br />One character has a dream in which a tide covers the entirety of the land where she live and when it reaches her doorstep, the hem of her dress is soaked with blood. She later has another dream in which she is standing side-by-side with a French soldier who has a bloody wound in his shoulder, but this wound is not described any more than what I just wrote.<br /><br /><b>Swearing:</b> After hearing news about the killing of women and children overseas, Walter cries out, â€œOh God, no!â€ several times. In the context I wasnâ€™t sure if he was actually crying out to God, or if he was using the Lordâ€™s name in vain.<br /><br />Other than that, the only other â€œswearingâ€ is the use of â€œdarnâ€ and â€œdarnedâ€ which Susan considers swears. Miss Oliver also threatens to swear, wondering if it would help her feel better when she gets so upset over the war news, and several times says something along the lines of, â€œBut donâ€™t you think it would feel good just to say dâ€”â€ and then Susan cuts her off before she can say whatever word was intended.<br /><br /><b>Romantic content:</b> Nothing sexual to be found here. Only a mild, chaste romance between Rilla and her sweetheart and, one undescribed kiss between them, and a promise that she wonâ€™t kiss anyone else while heâ€™s gone, to which she remains true. </span>

<div id="review_78646027" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78646027" />
    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78646027?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=l-m-montgomery">l-m-montgomery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78646027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17135675934446009143">Now I do know and realise that I am probably going to be seriously offending and angering some if not even rather many readers (and likely also more than a number of Goodreads friends) when I state that I absolutely and utterly despise L.M. Montgomery's <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> (and quite with every fibre of my being). For while, yes indeed, I do well know that the novel was written not only about WWI but also basically immediately post WWI and that it is and should therefore be considered an object (</span>
  <span id="freeText17135675934446009143" style="display:none">Now I do know and realise that I am probably going to be seriously offending and angering some if not even rather many readers (and likely also more than a number of Goodreads friends) when I state that I absolutely and utterly despise L.M. Montgomery's <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> (and quite with every fibre of my being). For while, yes indeed, I do well know that the novel was written not only about WWI but also basically immediately post WWI and that it is and should therefore be considered an object (a piece of fiction) of its time and place with its blatant anti-German sentiment and ultra nationalism, as a person of German background who has for one often been bullied and harassed because of her ethnicity and who has for two also read a goodly number of both British and German WWI novels that while definitely overly nationalistic were (are) still not only and simply diatribes of ethnic and cultural intolerance, I really and truly emotionally, personally cannot in any way stand and accept in particular the bigotry and racism against anything even remotely German shown and presented in <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> by the Blythe Family housekeeper Susan Baker (and actually even more that there is NEVER really ANY type of actual criticism of Susan Baker and her radical hatreds by the author, by Lucy Maud Montgomery, which sadly and frustratingly does make me think that the former, that Susan Baker as a character is likely very much a mirror image of L.M. Montgomery and her own perceptions and attitudes). And sorry, (but actually no, I guess I am not really all that sorry and do stand by my attitude of anger) but whenever I do read (or try to read) <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u>, although the novel is definitely emotionally charged, well written and yes definitely tugs at one's heart especially with regard to Walter Blythe's death in combat and how this affects everyone, emotionally and very much personally, I indeed consider <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> in many ways, in most ways quite as racist and as nastily intolerant as Montgomery's anti-Native Canadian short story, as her anti First Nations ranting tirade of <i>Tannis of the Flats</i> (which is one of the only L.M. Montgomery pieces of short fiction that I have absolutely and viscerally hated and which I also generally skip when rereading the author's <u>Further Chronicles of Avonlea</u>). <br /><br />And finally, while I would NOT go so far as to not recommend <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u>, I do leave the (I believe necessary) caveat that in my humble opinion, the Ã¼ber-nationalistic tendencies of the novel (and especially the casual acceptance of this by almost everyone, including the author, including L.M. Montgomery herself) do need to be discussed and indeed also condemned. As honestly, especially Susan Baker, she uses throughout <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> quite the SAME type of rhetoric against ALL Germans that the Nazis just a decade or so later were using against Jews, against Gypsies, basically against anyone not German, and I for one do find this both troubling and offensive (especially since not only the majority of reviewers but sadly also far far too many academic analyses of <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> seem either completely and blissfully unaware of or would rather ignore and not consider this to be a potential and possible problem and issue). <br /><br />So therefore, while I do from a stylistic point of view and departure (and also I guess to a certain extent from a historic realism point of view) both accept and even grudgingly commend L.M. Montgomery for having with her <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> penned a both sadly emotional and often beautiful story of love, loss and the home front, I still cannot and will not give more than one star to a novel that is basically full to the proverbial rim with vile hatred and nastiness towards and for me simply because of my ethnicity and background as a German, and especially since <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> is or at least seems so universally loved and considered acceptable in every way (but honestly and in my own and humble opinion, if or when those readers who absolutely adore <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> were to peruse a German WWI account with even just some minor and slight vestiges of that same bigotry, nationalism and racial anger presented and featured, they would more than likely lift their heads and rightfully, with justification collectively howl in and with angry protest, even as <u>Rilla of Ingleside</u> and especially Susan Baker's uncritically and even by L.M. Montgomery generally positively shown and depicted viciousness and angry outbursts against anything and anyone even remotely German seem totally alright to and for them).</span>
  <a data-text-id="17135675934446009143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2420893346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/6145295-rilla-of-ingleside">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=war">war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=family-based-fiction">family-based-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2420893346" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6663136008381513710"><b>"Before this war is over," he said - or something said through his lips - "every man and woman and child in Canada will feel it - you, Mary, will feel it - feel it to your heart's core. You will weep tears of blood over it. The Piper has come - and he will pipe until every corner of the world has heard his awful and irresistible music. It will be years before the dance of death is over -years, Mary. And in those years millions of hearts will break."</b><br/><br/> At first, everyone is more concerned with loca</span>
  <span id="freeText6663136008381513710" style="display:none"><b>"Before this war is over," he said - or something said through his lips - "every man and woman and child in Canada will feel it - you, Mary, will feel it - feel it to your heart's core. You will weep tears of blood over it. The Piper has come - and he will pipe until every corner of the world has heard his awful and irresistible music. It will be years before the dance of death is over -years, Mary. And in those years millions of hearts will break."</b><br /><br /> At first, everyone is more concerned with local gossip than the fact that some "Archduke Ferdinand or other" has been assassinated in a place called Sarajevo, but soon the affairs of the world have intruded into the quiet, peaceful village of Glen St. Mary. <br /><br /> <b>"England declared war on Germany today," said Jack Elliot slowly. "The news came by wire just as I left town."</b><br /><br /> And before long . . . <br /><br /> <b>"They are calling for volunteers in town, father," said Jem. "Scores have joined up already. I'm going in tonight to enlist."</b><br /><br /> Anne can do little but watch as her oldest son goes to war.<br /><br /> <b>". . . I am determined that I will send my boy off tomorrow with a smile. He shall not carry away with him the remembrance of a weak mother who had not the courage to send when he had the courage to go."</b><br /><br />And, so the womenfolk put on their brave faces, and begin their busy vigil. There are socks to knit, hastily arranged marriages to plan, and war babies to care for. Rilla, Anne's youngest child, grows from a rather spoiled fifteen-year-old into a caring and responsible young woman. <br /><br /><b>"<i>Our</i> sacrifice is greater than <i>his</i>," cried Rilla passionately. "Our boys give only <i>themselves</i>. <i>We</i> give them."</b><br /><br />Then Gilbert relates the devastating news that changes so many lives forever . . .<br /><br />I enjoyed this visit into Anne's world, though there is an incident of religious nutjobbery - <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[A young boy drowns his kitten in the hopes that his sacrifice will make God bring Jem back home safely. Shudder. But, everyone in the novel seems to think it's a touching gesture. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> - that I found rather disturbing, and must have managed to block out the other two times I read this book. Thankfully, Little Dog Monday, with his devoted Greyfriar's Bobby stunt, makes up for this, and his story made me sob yet again. <br /><br />This is a wonderful culmination to the series. It is an ending, yes, but somehow Montgomery leaves us feeling that life will go on for this family, even though we no longer get to be a part of it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6663136008381513710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_475010277" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475010277" />
    <a title="Darcy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3380448-darcy"><img alt="Darcy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432680736p2/3380448.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475010277?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darcy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darcy" href="/user/show/3380448-darcy">Darcy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475010277" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16520827888319734962">It was with great relief and disappointment that I finally closed the last volume of the Anne series. I'm afraid I found this volume a colossal bore. Rilla, cute as she is, was not featured as prominently as the title would suggest; nor was her love story. Set during WWI, Rilla of Ingleside chronicles the entire duration of the war and how it effected those in Canada. By the time they made it to 1918 I was praying for the war to end so the dang book would end. The scenes hardly varied. It was a </span>
  <span id="freeText16520827888319734962" style="display:none">It was with great relief and disappointment that I finally closed the last volume of the Anne series. I'm afraid I found this volume a colossal bore. Rilla, cute as she is, was not featured as prominently as the title would suggest; nor was her love story. Set during WWI, Rilla of Ingleside chronicles the entire duration of the war and how it effected those in Canada. By the time they made it to 1918 I was praying for the war to end so the dang book would end. The scenes hardly varied. It was a continuous dull poetic procession of Rilla crying in Rainbow Valley, writing about the war in her journal or in letters to the boys on the front, Walter, the poet-phrophet turned soldier blathering on about his cowardice, and women sitting around sewing and discussing the war. I guess Montgomery must have thought Susan, the Blythe's 60-year-old housemaid, was quite a provocative character, because she kept up a steady flow of opinions on the war and their stupid cat that made me want to poke my eyes out. <br /><br />I've noticed that Montgomery is better at character sketches than she is at prolonged character development, and like many of the books in this series, part of the problem is that it focused on random happenings with random neighbors that I cared little about. <br /><br />A few thoughts on Anne. About halfway through the series I realized that Anne was receding into motherhood and becoming nothing but a stolid matronly figure amongst more spirited beings. Once she enters motherhood she isn't allowed to dream, to write, to be funny or fanciful. She becomes sentimental and adored as a mother, but almost completely unrecognizable as the Anne we know and love. I suppose this is the product of the Victorian age - proper women don't dream beyond the confines of their domestic sphere and once Anne is firmly planted in her homely sphere, well, adventures of any kind are out of the question. <br /><br />Maybe what we love about Anne just doesn't translate into adulthood. A child who talks too much is cute and bright. An adult who won't shut up is annoying. A young girl's romantic longings are charming, but they don't exactly have a place in a married woman's life. And Gilbert, the hero of our romance, becomes about as exciting mushed peas. <br /><br />I'm glad I finished the series, but I desperately wish this series had ended with a more satisfying book.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16520827888319734962" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_136578450" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136578450" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4148588-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579896827p2/4148588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136578450?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/4148588-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670065196" href="/book/show/9687998-rilla-of-ingleside">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4148588-elizabeth?shelf=canadian-fiction">canadian-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4148588-elizabeth?shelf=children-s-fiction">children-s-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4148588-elizabeth?shelf=2011">2011</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136578450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4238268540943975410">It was recently drawn to my attention by a fellow Goodreads reader that the editions of <i>Rilla of Ingleside</i>, for which we had become accustomed, are abridged versions of the original edition that L.M. Montgomery published. Somehow along the way, an abridged edition appeared through an Amercian publishing house and that abridged version became the standard (accidentally). As a result, I was curious to discover what jewels of <i>Rilla of Ingleside</i> I was missing. While, I feel self-satisfied in the kno</span>
  <span id="freeText4238268540943975410" style="display:none">It was recently drawn to my attention by a fellow Goodreads reader that the editions of <i>Rilla of Ingleside</i>, for which we had become accustomed, are abridged versions of the original edition that L.M. Montgomery published. Somehow along the way, an abridged edition appeared through an Amercian publishing house and that abridged version became the standard (accidentally). As a result, I was curious to discover what jewels of <i>Rilla of Ingleside</i> I was missing. While, I feel self-satisfied in the knowledge of having truly read the complete version of <i>RofI</i>, I don't think I would have noticed any difference between my 1980s edition and the new complete version had I not known. Nevertheless, my journey with the Blythe family through WWI was still provoking, tearful, and delightful all over again. In addition, the newly discovered and restored version(edited by Benjamin Lefebvre and Andrea McKenzie) includes: a glossary, a brief history of WWI and two war poems written by L.M. Montgomery. Therefore, I do recommend that any L.M. Montgomery fan (or anyone who has not already discovered <i>Rilla of Ingleside</i>) pick up a copy of this edition immediately. It's a book that will add a certain charm to anyone's collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4238268540943975410" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1295920213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1295920213" />
    <a title="Olivia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43582060-olivia"><img alt="Olivia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604018166p2/43582060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1295920213?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia" href="/user/show/43582060-olivia">Olivia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43582060-olivia?shelf=my-real-bookshelf">my-real-bookshelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43582060-olivia?shelf=mid-teens-15-16">mid-teens-15-16</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43582060-olivia?shelf=world-war-1">world-war-1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43582060-olivia?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1295920213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13207311108439692633">2017 update: The more I've read this, the more I appreciate the metaphors, every day life of each characters, descriptions, laughter, and tears throughout. This book will never grow old for me. It's a dear friend :)<br/>***<br/>This is the most amazing, sweetest, heart-wrenching books I've read in my life. I've read it at least ten times (maybe more ;)) and I <b>love</b> it every. single. time.<br/><br/>This is a book that can be enjoyable read on its on, but it is ever so much better if you've read the whole series. So r</span>
  <span id="freeText13207311108439692633" style="display:none">2017 update: The more I've read this, the more I appreciate the metaphors, every day life of each characters, descriptions, laughter, and tears throughout. This book will never grow old for me. It's a dear friend :)<br />***<br />This is the most amazing, sweetest, heart-wrenching books I've read in my life. I've read it at least ten times (maybe more ;)) and I <b>love</b> it every. single. time.<br /><br />This is a book that can be enjoyable read on its on, but it is ever so much better if you've read the whole series. So read the whole series...and then just sigh with happiness, true bliss, when you read this one.<br /><br />Why I LOVE about this book:<br />-The characters are whimsical and delightful. L.M. Montgomery explains simple ordinary things as hilarious and interesting. She makes you smile and cry with one sentence.<br /><br />-Susan. She is my all time favorite character. Her mockery of the war and the leaders of the country made me smirk more than once. And then when daylight savings time is put into place she is adamant that it is against the Bible. Her love of Shirley, her devotion to the Blythes, and her stern ways make you simply want to hug her. She's so down to earth.<br /><br />-I'm afraid all my loves are the characters themselves. Walter Blythe should get his own review. He makes me want to soooob....He is amazing. My heart wrenched for him. He hated the war, but duty came first. Duty because it was the right thing to do. Sigghhh.<br /><br />-Kenneth Ford. Such a handsome fellow...and I especially like him because he is the son of the Ford's (whose love story is introduced in Anne's House of Dreams). I love how he calls Rilla, Rilla-my-Rilla. On this same note...I LOVE the scene when he has came to tell Rilla goodbye and Susan starts chatting about when she spanked him. I just died!!! And he is so serious and grim, but wants to burst out laughing the whole time.<br /><br />-And Rilla. I love how she learns to do her part for the war effort. And how she learns to love the little baby from the soup tureen. Yes, she is a character full of mistakes, but oh so dear. And her lisp. Hehe. I LOVE the last two sentences in the book and I quote them all the time (oh the calamity of lisping at the worst of times!!):<br /><br />"Is it Rilla-<i>my</i>-Rilla?"<br />"Yeth," said Rilla.<br /><br />-The setting. I have fallen in love with the World War 1 setting (probably because of this book)! Instead of showing the men fighting, it focuses on those left at home.<br /><br />Other thoughts:<br />-There are a couple swear words, but not more than a half of dozen. The middle is a little slow with all the fellows gone, but not enough to bore me, especially when Susan's around ;) There is a brief kiss in the middle of the book, but it's sweet and not detailed at all. One thing I would have liked is for the book to never end.<br /><br />I heartily LOVE LOVE LOVE this book. I review does not do justice to it. Go grab a copy and then fall in love with it too ;)</span>
  <a data-text-id="13207311108439692633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2369942140" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2369942140" />
    <a title="Britany" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5550397-britany"><img alt="Britany" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533999441p2/5550397.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2369942140?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Britany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Britany" href="/user/show/5550397-britany">Britany</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4898903072123965514"><b>Oh Rilla-my-Rilla. </b><br/><br/>This series has had its ups and downs, but this one was worth all the pain. This book was perfection. Told from Rilla Blythe's point of view in diary format, we start in early 1914 and she is carefree, frivolous and worried about what hat she'll wear to the dance. Enter the assassination of Franz Ferdinand and suddenly Ingleside finds itself ensconced in WW1. <br/><br/>This book had everything I need and never knew I wanted from this series.   <span id="freeText4898903072123965514" style="display:none"><b>Oh Rilla-my-Rilla. </b><br /><br />This series has had its ups and downs, but this one was worth all the pain. This book was perfection. Told from Rilla Blythe's point of view in diary format, we start in early 1914 and she is carefree, frivolous and worried about what hat she'll wear to the dance. Enter the assassination of Franz Ferdinand and suddenly Ingleside finds itself ensconced in WW1. <br /><br />This book had everything I need and never knew I wanted from this series. Completely takes the cake for my absolute favorite "Anne" book. I laughed, I cried, and I couldn't get enough from these characters. Rilla really stepped up to the plate with her antics and the raising of her war-baby Jims. This book found a very harsh reality in that all the boys enlisted and poor Dog Monday sat at the train station waiting for his soldier to return. I even loved the awful Mr Hyde (the cat!)- and Susan sealed her fate in my heart. <br /><br />If you haven't made it this far in the series, do yourself a favor and go pick this one up right now (I'm serious... what are you waiting for?)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4324379301462594620">The final book in the Anne of Green Gables series makes a solemn capstone to an amazing run. My Dad bought me these books when I was little on the condition that I read all of them, and I've just now fulfilled that promise.<br/><br/>Covering the duration of World War One, this book, along with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77394.Anne_s_House_of_Dreams__Anne_of_Green_Gables_No__5_' title='Anne's House of Dreams (Anne of Green Gables No. 5) by L.M.   <span id="freeText4324379301462594620" style="display:none">The final book in the Anne of Green Gables series makes a solemn capstone to an amazing run. My Dad bought me these books when I was little on the condition that I read all of them, and I've just now fulfilled that promise.<br /><br />Covering the duration of World War One, this book, along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77394.Anne_s_House_of_Dreams__Anne_of_Green_Gables_No__5_" title="Anne's House of Dreams (Anne of Green Gables No. 5) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne's House of Dreams</a>, is definitely one of the saddest in the series. Yet these two books give the characters of Anne and her children, including the title character, her youngest daughter Rilla, a more rounded feel. <br /><br />The story left me with a sense that the soul can bear a lot more suffering than any of us usually think it can. There is something terrible in that, since the last thing any of us want to experience is <i>more</i> suffering, but also something hopeful, since it helps us to understand that we can live through it and have a sense of happiness again. Maybe not the same unburdened happiness that we used to have--but happiness nonetheless.<br /><br />Although the book dragged in parts (as I'm sure real life did in those times as people waited desperately for word of their loved ones at the western front), any book that so unapologetically forces me to read it in two days and makes me cry deserves five stars.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1231684486663469314"><b>Full review now posted!</b><br/><br/>I DID IT! I actually managed to read all the way through the Anne of Green Gables series, all 8 books worth. Theyâ€™re not long books, and 8 might not sound like very many, but these are still technically classics and that would a good bit of classic reading in a row. While the books werenâ€™t all as wonderful as the first, none of them were bad and I feel like reading the series in its entirety was worthwhile.<br/><br/>Out of all of the books, this last one was probably my least favori</span>
  <span id="freeText1231684486663469314" style="display:none"><b>Full review now posted!</b><br /><br />I DID IT! I actually managed to read all the way through the Anne of Green Gables series, all 8 books worth. Theyâ€™re not long books, and 8 might not sound like very many, but these are still technically classics and that would a good bit of classic reading in a row. While the books werenâ€™t all as wonderful as the first, none of them were bad and I feel like reading the series in its entirety was worthwhile.<br /><br />Out of all of the books, this last one was probably my least favorite. It was much more solemn than those preceding it, as it took place during the First World War. Getting a view of the war from the Canadian Homefront, and through the eyes of Anneâ€™s youngest daughter as sheâ€™s on the cusp of womanhood, was very different and definitely interesting. We donâ€™t often give much thought to how wartime affects life for those left behind at home after the soldiers ship out, especially those in countries outside our own that donâ€™t see any fighting on their own soil. <br /><br />Rilla, Anneâ€™s youngest, has her life radically altered just as she enters her fifteenth year. Sheâ€™s a fun-loving girl who loves to laugh, and has no desire to plan for the future. The night of her first â€œgrown upâ€ party ends with the announcement that war has been declared in Europe. Before she can wrap her mind around whatâ€™s happening, every young man in her life is gone, fighting a war on foreign shore. Rilla has to grow up. She rises to the occasion and becomes a young woman that the whole community can be proud of.<br /><br />As with all the books, there are still funny situations. Rilla adopts a little war-baby, and her efforts with him add some much needed levity. There was also a faithful little dog who refused to leave the train station until his master came home from the war, which was a heartwarming addition. But as I said earlier, there was a solemnity to this book not present (as often) in the others due the addition of war into the plot. Montgomery did a very good job of capturing the tension and strain of those who have to stay home and just wait on news, and a masterful job of showing characters who have to bridge the divide between mourning and moving on with their lives.<br /><br />Iâ€™m very glad that I can now say that Iâ€™ve read the Anne of Green Gables series in its entirety. I think itâ€™s helped me develop a classic habit, which Iâ€™m going to do my best to maintain by always having some classic Iâ€™m reading, even if Iâ€™m only reading a chapter or two a day in addition to the other books Iâ€™m reading. It was a practice in endurance, but I feel like it paid off!</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kris" href="/user/show/2125614-kris">kris</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer257898140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18403760244866381094">Rilla Blythe is very happily 15 (GOING ON 16) when the world goes to war in 1914 and she comes into her own between watching her brothers and neighbors march off to battle, adopting a war-baby, organizing the local Junior Red Cross, and doing her best to keep the faith. <br/><br/>1. I'M NOT CRYING: I'VE JUST BEEN CUTTING ONIONS. (I'M MAKING A LASAGNAâ€”FOR <i>ONE</i>.)<br/><br/>2. For as period-specific that this book is, there is something timeless about its story all the same. That sensation of worry and tension and growi</span>
  <span id="freeText18403760244866381094" style="display:none">Rilla Blythe is very happily 15 (GOING ON 16) when the world goes to war in 1914 and she comes into her own between watching her brothers and neighbors march off to battle, adopting a war-baby, organizing the local Junior Red Cross, and doing her best to keep the faith. <br /><br />1. I'M NOT CRYING: I'VE JUST BEEN CUTTING ONIONS. (I'M MAKING A LASAGNAâ€”FOR <i>ONE</i>.)<br /><br />2. For as period-specific that this book is, there is something timeless about its story all the same. That sensation of worry and tension and growing accustomed to it and working around it and <i>surviving</i> even in the face of devastation and loss and mourning. And it's powerful, especially with the Centennial Commemoration of Armistice Day. The world <i>changed</i> and seeing the familiar, friendly world of Anne Shirley melting into the devastation of a century that saw global war is...haunting and hard. <br /><br />3. WALTER BLYTHE. <br /><br />4. GODDAMNED DOG MONDAY. <br /><br />5. Further violence towards cats tho; Montgomery why must you do me dirty like this. <br /><br />6. I very much want Gertrude Oliver's book because her caustic cynicism/optimism blend was so relatable and honest and ME.<br /><br />7. This complaint is going to seem silly because this book is clearly Rilla's book (which I wouldn't change for anything; I think the story transitioning to the next generation is probably the only way to end Anne's series), BUT: I missed Anne Blythe <i>nee</i> Shirley in this. She is, in some ways, a stranger, the old married matriarch of the Ingleside home. And there are moments that attempt to remind us that she's still very much Anne of Green Gables underneath it all, but those moments felt sloppy and unearned. <br /><br />(Don't even get me started on Susan interrupting most of those moments. I mean, there is literally one scene where she stands at the foot of "Mrs. Dr. dear"'s bed to announce some war news!! I MEAN I COMPLAIN CONSISTENTLY ABOUT THE LACK OF KISSING IN THESE BOOKS BUT WHAT FURTHER EVIDENCE DO YOU NEED THAT THE HOUSE OF BLYTHE HAS APPARENTLY GIVEN UP ON MORE THAN KISSING??? HERE I MOURN.)<br /><br />(Truly and honestly: I think a book truly about "old married Anne Shirley" would be kind of fantastic??)<br /><br />Also read: 2006</span>

<div id="review_20838816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/20838816" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1096902-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1208613312p2/1096902.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/20838816?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/1096902-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096902-elizabeth?shelf=children-teen-fiction">children-teen-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1096902-elizabeth?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer20838816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5086168628195532616">What made the Anne series so enjoyable was that Anne was a very three-dimensional character. She had spunk, she was stubborn, she was whimsical, she knew how to love and she knew what loss was. Rilla is not a three-dimensional character, or at least not to the same extent that her mother was. It's been ages since I read these books, and maybe I'd enjoy this one more if I were to read it again as an adult, but by the time I get through <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77391.Anne_of_Ingleside__Anne_of_Green_Gables___6_' title='Anne of Ingleside (Anne of Green Gables, #6) by L.M. Montgomery' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anne of Ingleside</a>, I'm so entirely fed up with the series tha</span>
  <span id="freeText5086168628195532616" style="display:none">What made the Anne series so enjoyable was that Anne was a very three-dimensional character. She had spunk, she was stubborn, she was whimsical, she knew how to love and she knew what loss was. Rilla is not a three-dimensional character, or at least not to the same extent that her mother was. It's been ages since I read these books, and maybe I'd enjoy this one more if I were to read it again as an adult, but by the time I get through <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77391.Anne_of_Ingleside__Anne_of_Green_Gables___6_" title="Anne of Ingleside (Anne of Green Gables, #6) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne of Ingleside</a>, I'm so entirely fed up with the series that I can't be bothered to continue.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5086168628195532616" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2479587" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2479587" />
    <a title="Kat Leache" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/80124-kat-leache"><img alt="Kat Leache" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378824553p2/80124.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2479587?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat Leache" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat Leache" href="/user/show/80124-kat-leache">Kat Leache</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80124-kat-leache?shelf=re-readfavorites">re-readfavorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2479587" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14902708425514020967">My faaaaavorite Anne book--it focuses on her youngest child, Rilla, and it's set during WWI (the series is set in Canada, who entered the war with England, so it was a much longer and painful ordeal for them than for the US). Rilla is a silly young 15 year old when the war begins--by the end she's an amazing and strong woman. This book is wonderful! I'm regularly tearing up on my lunchbreak reading this one.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2880139795" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2880139795" />
    <a title="Kavita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita"><img alt="Kavita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315387023p2/5858699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2880139795?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kavita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kavita" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita">Kavita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=canada">canada</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=not-recommended">not-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=mary-sue-gary-stu">mary-sue-gary-stu</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=world-war-one">world-war-one</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2880139795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11701746113978629399"><i>Rilla of Ingleside</i> is so boring that it took me more than a month to read a measly 250 pages. And not only that, it gave me a distaste to read anything else, so I actually didn't read much over the last month while I struggled with this. I can't think of a worse book that I have read recently and I wouldn't recommend it to my worst enemy.<br/><br/>Rilla, the youngest of the Blythe children, is now a teenager and World War I is looming. Suddenly all the young men in the area are cracking jokes about killin</span>
  <span id="freeText11701746113978629399" style="display:none"><i>Rilla of Ingleside</i> is so boring that it took me more than a month to read a measly 250 pages. And not only that, it gave me a distaste to read anything else, so I actually didn't read much over the last month while I struggled with this. I can't think of a worse book that I have read recently and I wouldn't recommend it to my worst enemy.<br /><br />Rilla, the youngest of the Blythe children, is now a teenager and World War I is looming. Suddenly all the young men in the area are cracking jokes about killing Germans and yearning to go and kill random people they have never met despite their country not being under attack. Apparently, some WASP thing where "old grey mother of the northern sea" must be defended at all costs. Excuse me while I go vomit! What a fucking con! In fact, Jem, the eldest son of Anne, and apparently a blithering idiot, is fairly going orgasmic at the thought of war. <br /><br /><i>"We're the cubsâ€”we've got to pitch in tooth and claw if it comes to a family row," Jem went on cheerfully, rumpling up his red curls with a strong, lean, sensitive brown handâ€”the hand of the born surgeon, his father often thought. "What an adventure it would be! But I suppose Grey or some of those wary old chaps will patch matters up at the eleventh hour. It'll be a rotten shame if they leave France in the lurch, though. If they don't, we'll see some fun. Well, I suppose it's time to get ready for the spree at the light."</i><br /><br />Anne is no better. Though not wildly ecstatic unlike the youngsters, she is sad but sees no reason to not send her sons out to sacrifice them - for England! WTF? WASP idiots! This might be the sign of the times, but don't tell me there were not people who didn't want to join the war in far-off Europe. Canada literally had riots over this issue! Montgomery, and by extension, Anne and her family, were on the wrong side of history. The only pacifist in the entire village was soon shut down.<br /><br />I now dislike Anne, her family, her neighbours, and the entire PEI community. What a waste of my time to read EIGHT books about a character who becomes a boring, brainless housewife, promoting war and revelling in the "bravery" of her sons. Poor young men conned by everyone to go fight for another country! <br /><br />But what I feel for Anne is nowhere close to the burning hatred I feel for Susan Baker. She has been in the forefront in the last two books and I was already heartily tired of her. In this book, the disgusting woman keeps up an unbearable commentary of brainless comments regarding politics and war and I couldn't stand the humiliation of a female character being so empty-headed. Even if the most sexist man had to create a female character, he can't do better than copy Susan. Ugh! She has also by now completely encroached on the Blythe family and it looks like she will be spending the rest of her life there. <br /><br />As if this is not all, the other major plot of the book is Rilla bringing home an abandoned baby. Her father (a doctor!) tells her she has to take care of it or it will be sent away to die. WTF? Hippocratic oath, anyone? Apparently Gilbert, boy has never heard of it! Also, please, it's child abuse to expect a child to be responsible for a baby. Adults should be in charge of babies. I disliked this plot and while it did give Rilla some character growth, I just hated the adult characters more and more. <br /><br />Then there was a pretty random romance plot between Rilla and some boy, who was apparently also masturbating at the mention of war. Not much time was given to it and I was a little confused that the two got engaged at the end. <br /><br />By the end of it all, I was wishing the Germans had dropped a large bomb on this fictional PEI and killed all these jerks who thought war was so great. That would be the only ending that would have redeemed all the time I wasted reading all these books. <br /><br />As a series, I think only the first book is superlative and must be read. The struggles of an orphan girl finding sanctuary and love is beautifully depicted. Then Anne's exploits in the next three books can be interesting but they don't really have the same charm. The series should have ended with Anne's marriage because Anne doesn't really figure much after she is married and the unbearable Susan Baker takes over the star role, which really brings down the quality of these books. Book 5 is maudlin and overly dramatic. Book 6 is all about Anne's kids and book 7 about random people with no connection to the rest of the series. Book 8 is the worst of the lot.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11701746113978629399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Allie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/887727-allie"><img alt="Allie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253278092p2/887727.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/43700143?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allie" href="/user/show/887727-allie">Allie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
